<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Aegilops tauschii genome reveals multiple impacts of transposons"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2017 3:12"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-11-20"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Wheat is an important global crop with an extremely large and complex genome that contains more transposable elements (TEs) than any other known crop species. Here, we generated a chromosome-scale, high-quality reference genome of Aegilops tauschii, the donor of the wheat D genome, in which 92.5% sequences have been anchored to chromosomes. Using this assembly, we accurately characterized genic loci, gene expression, pseudogenes, methylation, recombination ratios, microRNAs and especially TEs on chromosomes. In addition to the discovery of a wave of very recent gene duplications, we detected that TEs occurred in about half of the genes, and found that such genes are expressed at lower levels than those without TEs, presumably because of their elevated methylation levels. We mapped all wheat molecular markers and constructed a high-resolution integrated genetic map corresponding to genome sequences, thereby placing previously detected agronomically important genes/quantitative trait loci&#160;(QTLs) on the Ae. tauschii genome for the first time. The wild grass Aegilops tauschii is a wheat progenitor. A high-quality genome sequence, along with methylome and transcriptome data, provides insights on domestication and the effect of transposons, and offers a resource for wheat improvement."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-8987-2782"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-8987-2782</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="the Chinese Academy of Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000000119573309, grid.9227.e, State Key Laboratory of Systematic and Evolutionary Botany, Institute of Botany, the Chinese Academy of Sciences, 100093, Beijing, China" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jizeng-Jia" data-author-popup="auth-Jizeng-Jia" data-corresp-id="c5">Jizeng Jia<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8671-2145"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-8671-2145</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Chinese Academy of Agricultural Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 0526 1937, grid.410727.7, Key Laboratory of Crop Gene Resources and Germplasm Enhancement, Ministry of Agriculture, The National Key Facility for Crop Gene Resources and Genetic Improvement, Institute of Crop Sciences, Chinese Academy of Agricultural Sciences, 100081, Beijing, China" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Wheat is an important global crop with an extremely large and complex genome that contains more transposable elements (TEs) than any other known crop species. Here, we generated a chromosome-scale, high-quality reference genome of <i>Aegilops tauschii</i>, the donor of the wheat D genome, in which 92.5% sequences have been anchored to chromosomes. Using this assembly, we accurately characterized genic loci, gene expression, pseudogenes, methylation, recombination ratios, microRNAs and especially TEs on chromosomes. In addition to the discovery of a wave of very recent gene duplications, we detected that TEs occurred in about half of the genes, and found that such genes are expressed at lower levels than those without TEs, presumably because of their elevated methylation levels. We mapped all wheat molecular markers and constructed a high-resolution integrated genetic map corresponding to genome sequences, thereby placing previously detected agronomically important genes/quantitative trait loci (QTLs) on the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome for the first time.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">
                        
                            
    
    <div class="c-pdf-download u-clear-both">
        <a href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8.pdf" class="c-pdf-download__link" data-article-pdf="true" data-readcube-pdf-url="true" data-test="download-pdf" data-draft-ignore="true" data-track="click" data-track-action="download pdf" data-track-label="link" data-track-external download>
            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Wheat is one of the most important food crops in the world. It is also a species with an extremely large and complex genome that contains more TEs than any other known species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science &#xA;                           345, 1249721 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d40588e549">1</a></sup>. This has for some time caused wheat research to lag behind that of crops with smaller genomes like rice, sorghum, etc. TEs were discovered by Barbara McClintock in 1951<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="McClintock, B. Chromosome organization and genic expression. Cold Spring Harb. Symp. Quant. Biol. &#xA;                           16, 13–47 (1951)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d40588e553">2</a></sup>, but for about three decades dating from her discovery, TEs were largely thought to be ‘Junk DNA’, parasite genes in a host genome, or selfish DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Orgel, L. E. &amp; Crick, F. H. C. Selfish DNA: the ultimate parasite. Nature &#xA;                           284, 604–607 (1980)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d40588e557">3</a></sup>. TEs make up a large fraction of many plant genomes; indeed, they are the major determinant of genome size. For example, more than 80% of the genomes of maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science &#xA;                           326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d40588e561">4</a></sup> and wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science &#xA;                           345, 1249721 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d40588e565">1</a></sup> are composed of TEs. More recently, TEs have been recognized as important functional components of genomes. Four distinct functional contributions of TEs are now recognized, including their roles in determining genome size and rearrangements, in generating mutations, in altering chromosome architecture and in the regulation of gene expression. TEs are now recognized as an abundant and unexplored natural source of regulatory sequences for host genes, and TE biology has become an active research area in recent years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Feschotte, C., Jiang, N. &amp; Wessler, S. R. Plant transposable elements: where genetics meets genomics. Nat. Rev. Genet. &#xA;                           3, 329–341 (2002)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d40588e570">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Slotkin, R. K. &amp; Martienssen, R. Transposable elements and the epigenetic regulation of the genome. Nat. Rev. Genet. &#xA;                           8, 272–285 (2007)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d40588e570_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Feschotte, C. Transposable elements and the evolution of regulatory networks. Nat. Rev. Genet. &#xA;                           9, 397–405 (2008)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d40588e570_2">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lisch, D. Epigenetic regulation of transposable elements in plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           60, 43–66 (2009)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d40588e570_3">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bucher, E., Reinders, J. &amp; Mirouze, M. Epigenetic control of transposon transcription and mobility in Arabidopsis. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           15, 503–510 (2012)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d40588e570_4">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Lisch, D. Regulation of the mutator system of transposons in maize. Methods Mol. Biol. &#xA;                           1057, 123–142 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d40588e573">10</a></sup>.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Sequencing technologies such as Illumina HiSeq X Ten and PacBio RS II sequencing, and new library construction methods such as 10x Genomics, as well as new assembly techniques such as DeNovoMAGIC2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lu, F. et al. High-resolution genetic mapping of maize pan-genome sequence anchors. Nat. Commun. &#xA;                           6, 6914 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d40588e580">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science &#xA;                           357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d40588e583">12</a></sup>, are now significantly accelerating the progress of wheat genome sequencing efforts. Common wheat is a hexaploid species with A, B and D subgenomes. Whole genome shotgun sequencing of the Chinese Spring cultivar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Brenchley, R. et al. Analysis of the bread wheat genome using whole-genome shotgun sequencing. Nature &#xA;                           491, 705–710 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d40588e587">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science &#xA;                           345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d40588e590">14</a></sup> and its diploid ancestors <i>Triticum urartu</i>
                     <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ling, H. Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature &#xA;                           496, 87–90 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d40588e597">15</a></sup> and <i>Ae. tauschii</i>
                     <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature &#xA;                           496, 91–95 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d40588e605">16</a></sup> have been reported. Additionally, chromosome sorting was used to sequence chromosome 3B of the hexaploid wheat cultivar Chinese Spring, which advanced wheat research significantly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science &#xA;                           345, 1249721 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d40588e609">1</a></sup>. Although previous efforts generated draft genomes for diploid ancestors and for bread wheat, the majority of contigs could not be mapped to chromosomes in these assemblies. We previously constructed the draft genome of <i>Ae. tauschii</i>, and found that it was particularly rich in genes related to adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature &#xA;                           496, 91–95 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d40588e616">16</a></sup>. Here, we present a reference D genome in which more than 92.5% of the genome sequences are anchored to chromosomes. We mapped the global distribution of TEs and examined multiple functional impacts of TEs on D genome evolution, gene structure and gene expression.</p></div><section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec2">Genome assembly and feature annotation</h3><p>Over 778 Gb of short read sequences were generated using two Illumina sequencing platforms (HiSeq 2000 and HiSeq 2500) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">1</a>). Short reads were first assembled by DeNovoMAGIC2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lu, F. et al. High-resolution genetic mapping of maize pan-genome sequence anchors. Nat. Commun. &#xA;                           6, 6914 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d40588e634">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science &#xA;                           357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d40588e637">12</a></sup> using 450 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb and 8 kb libraries to generate the V1.0 assembly, which comprises 271,060 contigs (N50 = 50.3 kb) and 117,344 scaffolds (N50 = 6.8 Mb). The assembly was further elongated using SSPACE with 20 kb and 40 kb libraries to generate the V1.1 assembly (N50 = 13.1 Mb). Finally, we used long reads generated with the PacBio RS II sequencing platform to further improve the contig assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">2</a>). The final assembly (V1.2) comprises 188,412 contigs (N50 = 112.6 kb) and 112,517 scaffolds (N50 = 12.1 Mb) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">3</a>). Our assembly represents a greater than 210-fold improvement in contiguity compared with the previously published <i>Ae. tauschii</i> assembly reported by our research group in 2013<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature &#xA;                           496, 91–95 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d40588e651">16</a></sup> (see statistics for V0.1 in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">2</a>). The total scaffold length of the V1.2 assembly (4.31 Gb) spans 95.8% of the estimated genome size (4.5 Gb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature &#xA;                           496, 91–95 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d40588e658">16</a></sup>, and the largest 434 scaffolds cover 90% of the genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><p>A high-density genetic map containing 164,872 single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) loci spanning 1,153.6 cM in seven linkage groups was used to anchor the scaffolds to chromosomes. In total, 658 scaffolds were aligned and anchored to the genetic map, with a total length of 4.0 Gb, spanning 92.5% of the assembled genome; 97.9% of the aforementioned SNPs were placed on the scaffolds (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">4</a>). We confirmed that the sizes of the chromosomes in our assembly were consistent with the results of a cytogenetics analysis that we performed for this study: chromosomes 2, 3, 5 and 7 are relatively longer, but chromosomes 1, 4 and 6 are shorter (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">2</a>). Attesting to the quality of the assembly, Illumina paired end reads were mapped to our assembly and 99.96% of them could be mapped, which suggests that our assembly contains almost all of the information in the raw reads (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">6</a>). Completeness of gene regions was assessed using CEGMA (conserved core eukaryotic gene mapping approach) and BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single Copy Orthologs). Two hundred and forty-three of the 248 (97.9%) conserved core eukaryotic genes from CEGMA were captured in our assembly), and 240 (98.8%) of these were complete. BUSCO analysis showed that 97% of the plant single-copy orthologues were complete. We also mapped 10,748 EST sequences generated from our <i>Ae. tauschii</i> full-length cDNA libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature &#xA;                           496, 91–95 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d40588e684">16</a></sup> to the assembly; 10,471 (97.4%) of these could be mapped to the scaffolds with greater than 90% coverage, which indicated that gene regions were almost complete in our assembly. To assess large-scale accuracy, we compared our assembly with the sequences of 17 BACs obtained either from the NCBI GenBank or from our in-house library. All of these BACs could be aligned to our assembly, in the correct order, with high sequence identity (15 of 17 alignments had identity &gt; 99%), and had greater than 97% coverage. Only two of the gaps located in genic regions, all other gaps on the BACs were intergenic sequence, repeats or N strings (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">10</a>). The accuracy of the assembly was further confirmed by contig sequences which were randomly selected from a genome version generated from PacBio data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. Hybrid assembly of the large and highly repetitive genome of Aegilops tauschii, a progenitor of bread wheat, with the MaSuRCA mega-reads algorithm. Genome Res. &#xA;                           27, 787–792 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d40588e694">17</a></sup>. All of the 120 selected PacBio Contigs could be unambiguously aligned to our assembly with sequence identity greater than 99.3% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">11</a>). These dramatic improvements in quality result from the use of emerging techniques like DeNovoMAGIC2, large insert mate-pair sequencing and PacBio, especially for TE-rich genomes. We annotated protein-coding genes (PCGs), RNA, pseudogenes and catalogued functional annotations for PCGs. Importantly, we also analysed the distribution of TEs on the seven chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig1">1</a>). The <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome sequence contains 42,828 PCGs, 98% of which were anchored to chromosomes in our assembly (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Tab1">1</a>); this is more than the 39,425 PCGs reported for Chinese Spring<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science &#xA;                           345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d40588e711">14</a></sup>. The average lengths, the number of exons and the GC content of the coding regions of the PCGs are similar to those of the genes in the genomes of other grass species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">19</a>). However, both the intron lengths and the intron GC content of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> PCGs are much larger than those in any other sequenced grass species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">19</a>). These differences are perhaps due to the dramatically higher number of TE insertions in the introns of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> PCGs. Of the PCGs, 92.6% were functionally annotated based on information from the NR (non-redundant database in NCBI), SwissProt, InterPro, Pfam and KEGG databases (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">20</a>). We identified 25,893 likely pseudogenes with premature stop codons or frameshift mutations, a much higher number than that in rice (1,439–5,608)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zou, C. et al. Evolutionary and expression signatures of pseudogenes in Arabidopsis and rice. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           151, 3–15 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d40588e731">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Thibaud-Nissen, F., Ouyang, S. &amp; Buell, C. R. Identification and characterization of pseudogenes in the rice gene complement. BMC Genomics &#xA;                           10, 317 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d40588e734">19</a></sup> or <i>Arabidopsis</i> (801–4,108)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zou, C. et al. Evolutionary and expression signatures of pseudogenes in Arabidopsis and rice. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           151, 3–15 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d40588e741">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Xiao, J. et al. Pseudogenes and their genome-wide prediction in plants. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 17, 1991 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d40588e744">20</a></sup>. When including gene fragments without disabling mutations in a broader pseudogene definition, the total number of pseudogenes reached 267,546 in the <i>Ae</i>. <i>tauschii</i> genome, which is two times larger than the number in the maize genome (B73 RefGen_v3, in the 5b + annotation build)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Law, M. et al. Automated update, revision, and quality control of the maize genome annotations using MAKER-P improves the B73 RefGen_v3 gene models and identifies new genes. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           167, 25–39 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d40588e755">21</a></sup>. Therefore, <i>Ae. tauschii</i> appears to be the plant species with the highest reported pseudogene content. In addition, 3,630 transfer RNA genes, 238 miRNA genes, 1,271 small nuclear RNA genes and 2,856 ribosomal RNA genes were predicted in the genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">22</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 The gene and TE distribution on the seven chromosomes of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                           <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Distribution of genomic features in the Ae. tauschii genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Distribution of genomic features in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0067-8/MediaObjects/41477_2017_67_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0067-8/MediaObjects/41477_2017_67_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Track a, the seven chromosomes. One scale label indicates 10 Mb. The black histogram indicates the distribution of two LTR-RTs (Quinta and Cereba); peaks indicate candidate centromere regions. Track b, miRNA. Track c, Gene density indicated by gene length/5 Mb. Track d, pseudogene density, indicated by gene length/5 Mb. Track e, recombination, measured by cM/Mb with 5 Mb bins. Track f, gene expression, calculated as the average reads per kilobase per million mapped reads (rpkm) in 5 Mb bins. Maximum rpkm from eight tissues and normalization of rpkm by log<sub>10</sub>(rpkm + 10<sup>-6</sup>). Track g, methylation, calculated by methylation base counts/C base counts in Mb bins. Only the most abundant CG methylation contexts are presented. Track h, total TE density across chromosomes, TE length/5 Mb. Track i, Gypsy. The distribution of Gypsy TE density across chromosomes: Gypsy length/5 Mb. Track j, Copia. The distribution of Copia density across chromosomes: Copia length/5 Mb. Track k, DNA TEs. The distribution of DNA transposon density across chromosomes: DNA transposon length/5 Mb.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Three whole genome duplication events have been identified in the evolutionary history of all of the sequenced grass genomes, including the <i>tau</i> event at ~150 million years ago (Ma), the <i>sigma</i> event at ~127 Ma and the <i>rho</i> event at ~70 Ma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science &#xA;                           326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1509">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Jiao, Y., Li, J., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Integrated syntenic and phylogenomic analyses reveal an ancient genome duplication in monocots. The Plant Cell &#xA;                           26, 2792–2802 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1512">22</a></sup>. To infer the evolutionary history of <i>Ae. tauschii</i>, we used MCScanx<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: A toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucl. Acids Res. &#xA;                           40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1520">23</a></sup> to detect syntenic genomic regions among <i>Ae. tauschii</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> and <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>; all of these intergenomic comparisons showed very strong co-linearity (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">12</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">14</a>), further indicating the high-quality of our <i>Ae. tauschii</i> assembly and PCG annotation. An intragenomic comparison of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> showed a relatively smaller number of syntenic regions than that in other sequenced grass genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">15</a>), with the largest syntenic region occurring between chromosomes 1 and 3. Interestingly, this region is also present in the respective syntenic regions of the genomes of five other sequenced grass genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig2">2b</a>). Genome alignments between <i>Ae. tauschii</i> and both <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i> revealed a clear 2-to-2 multiplicity ratio between orthologous regions, which supports the idea that the pan-cereal <i>rho</i> event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Tang, H., Bowers, J. E., Wang, X. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Angiosperm genome comparisons reveal early polyploidy in the monocot lineage. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA &#xA;                           107, 472–477 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1571">24</a></sup> was the most recent whole genome duplication event in the evolutionary history of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig2">2b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Aegilops genome evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Aegilops genome evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0067-8/MediaObjects/41477_2017_67_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0067-8/MediaObjects/41477_2017_67_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>
                                       <b>a</b>, Circos plot showing the syntenic regions in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome detected by MCScanx. <b>b</b>, Synteny comparisons of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> chromosomes 1 and 3 to the genomes of rice and sorghum. Quota ratios of the alignments are shown above the dot plots. <b>c</b>, <i>K</i>
                                       <sub>s</sub> value distributions of 9,569 <i>Aegilops</i> paralogues identified from best reciprocal BLASTP searches. Coloured lines superimposed on <i>K</i>
                                       <sub>s</sub> plots represent significant components identified via a likelihood mixture model implemented in EMMIX. Each component is written as ‘colour/mean <i>K</i>
                                       <sub>s</sub> value/proportion’, where colour is the component (curve) colour and proportion is the percentage of duplicated genes assigned to the identified component. There are four components: grey/0.03/0.04, red/0.23/0.44, green/0.72/0.45 and blue/1.71/0.07. The smaller plot represents the same distribution after removal of the tandem duplicates. The four components are grey/0.04/0.04, red/0.23/0.33, green/0.70/0.55 and blue/1.71/0.08. <b>d</b>, Density plot of <i>K</i>
                                       <sub>s</sub> values of best reciprocal BLASTP hits in the genomes of <i>Ae. tauschii, B. distachyon, O</i>. <i>sativa</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>Z. mays</i>. Density distribution of <i>K</i>
                                       <sub>s</sub> values of <i>Ae</i>. <i>tauschii</i> syntenic anchor gene pairs.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Transposable element analysis</h3><p>TEs account for fully 85.9% of the assembled sequence of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, similar to what occurs in bread wheat (chromosome 3B)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science &#xA;                           345, 1249721 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1681">1</a></sup> and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science &#xA;                           326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1685">4</a></sup>, but much higher than the TE content in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature &#xA;                           436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1689">25</a></sup>, sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature &#xA;                           457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1693">26</a></sup> or <i>B. distachyon</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature &#xA;                           463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1701">27</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">13</a>). Note that 85.9% TE content is much higher than what we previously estimated based on our <i>Ae. tauschii</i> V0.1 draft genome (62.3%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature &#xA;                           496, 91–95 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1711">16</a></sup>, and also higher than in Chinese Spring (76.6%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science &#xA;                           345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1715">14</a></sup>. This can be partly attributed to the greater than 210-fold improvement in contiguity of our new assembly. Retrotransposons and DNA transposons cover, respectively, 59.9% and 19.6% of the genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">14</a>). Long terminal repeats (LTRs) are the most abundant type of TE, covering 58% of the genome. Three superfamilies (Gypsy, 38.3%; CACTA, 16.8%; Copia, 16.5%) account for 71.7% of the total TE component (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">14</a>). Compared to both rice and maize, the proportion of CACTAs in <i>Ae. tauschii</i> is almost tenfold greater<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science &#xA;                           326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1729">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature &#xA;                           436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1732">25</a></sup>.</p><p>To further explore the proliferation in each TE superfamily, we here define ‘family’ according to the peptide sequence similarity of key transposase domains (threshold ≥ 90%; retrotransposase domain (RT) for retrotransposons and the catalytic domains of DD [E/D] (DDE) DNA transposons). In total, we identified 4,669 TE families (100,879 copies with complete transposase domains) among eight superfamilies/orders in our assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">15</a>). Consistent with the DNA-sequence-based TE annotation (above), our domain-based analysis showed that LTR retrotransposons were the most abundant type of TE, and CACTA elements were the most abundant type among the DNA transposons.</p><p>We identified 172 CACTA families that formed four clades in a phylogenetic tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig3">3a</a>). The four clades, Aet-CACTA-1 to Aet-CACTA-4, consisted of 66, 49, 38 and 19 families. To investigate their origins, we identified the CACTA families of five published grass genomes and combined them with the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> CACTAs to obtain a six-species CACTA data set containing 855 families (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">17</a>); the genomes included were common wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Brenchley, R. et al. Analysis of the bread wheat genome using whole-genome shotgun sequencing. Nature &#xA;                           491, 705–710 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1754">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science &#xA;                           345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1757">14</a></sup>, <i>Triticum urartu</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ling, H. Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature &#xA;                           496, 87–90 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1765">15</a></sup>, <i>B. distachyon</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature &#xA;                           463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1772">27</a></sup>, rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature &#xA;                           436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1776">25</a></sup> and sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature &#xA;                           457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1780">26</a></sup>. The topology of the neighbour-joining phylogenetic tree distinguished four clades of CACTAs in <i>Ae. tauschii</i>, and clearly suggested that the Aet-CACTA-4 clade originated in the Triticeae lineage, whereas the other three clades originated before the divergence of the major grass groups. A total of 19 families are included in Aet-CACTA-4, relatively fewer than in the three other clades. The centromere-enriched distribution of the Aet-CACTA-4 TEs in <i>Ae. tauschii</i> is distinct from the distributions of the other known CACTA subfamilies, which are more abundant in terminal, gene-rich regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig3">3b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Rapid proliferation of transposable elements."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Rapid proliferation of transposable elements.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0067-8/MediaObjects/41477_2017_67_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0067-8/MediaObjects/41477_2017_67_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>
                                       <b>a</b>, A neighbour-joining phylogeny of CACTA families. Four major clades with high bootstrap support are marked in different colours. Format of sequence ID: ‘f number 1–number 2’, where number 1 is the family ID and number 2 is the copy number of that family. For example, “f183 1639” indicates that family 183 has 1,639 copies. The black clade is the outgroup. The branch length is not proportional to the genetic distance. <b>b</b>, Chromosome-wide distribution of the high-copy-number families (copy-number ≥ 100) among the four major clades of CACTAs. The colour scheme is the same in <b>a</b> and <b>b.</b>
                                    </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Analysis of the evolutionary relationships of transposase domains showed that the high-copy-number families accounted for only a small proportion of all families, but 59.1% (2,829/4,782) of families had a copy number of 2 or 3 (we set the lowest copy number as 2 in this study) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">15</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">9</a>). For example, only eight out of 172 CACTA families were high-copy-number families (copy number &gt; 100), but these eight families (5% of all families) had 5,333 copies, representing 84% of all identified CACTA copies. A similar pattern was observed for LTR elements, where 99 (2.5%) of the high-copy-number families accounted for 65,912 (72.6%) of all identified LTR copies. Since CACTA and LTR elements account for over 70% of all <i>Ae</i>. <i>tauschii</i> TEs, this pattern suggests that a few dominant families might play key roles in recent genome structural evolution, supporting previous suppositions from a study of bread wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science &#xA;                           345, 1249721 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1842">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Devos, K. M. Grass genome organization and evolution. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           13, 139–145 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1845">28</a></sup>.</p><p>We next analysed the global distribution of TEs on chromosomes and found that they tended to increase in density along the chromosome from distal regions towards the centromere (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">19</a>). The distribution of Gypsy TEs is similar to that of total TEs, as indicated by a significant positive correlation (<i>r</i> = 0.92, <i>P</i> &lt; 10<sup>−13</sup>). Given that Gypsy TEs represent fully 38.3% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">14</a>) of total TEs, this high correlation is not surprising. However, the density of both Copia and CACTA TEs decreased from distal regions to centromeric/pericentromeric regions, showing a significant negative correlation with the distribution of total TEs and of Gypsy TEs (<i>P</i> &lt; 10<sup>−13</sup>). To explore the impact of TEs on the distribution of genomic features, we plotted the distributions of both gene and pseudogene density, and also examined gene expression, DNA methylation and recombination on every chromosome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig1">1</a>). We found that TEs are associated significantly with all of the other genomic features, highlighting the consequential impact of TEs on this genome. The density of Gypsy elements is significantly positively correlated with average gene expression levels, but is negatively correlated with both gene density and recombination. Similarly, negative correlations were observed for Copia and DNA TEs, although these trends were less obvious. All of these trends are consistent with the findings from an analysis of chromosome 3B of bread wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science &#xA;                           345, 1249721 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1879">1</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Impact of TEs on gene evolution and regulation</h3><p>Considering that 85.9% of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome is composed of TEs, we explored the impact of TEs on gene duplication, gene structure, methylation, expression and pseudogenization. We found a large number of recently duplicated genes (not resulting from whole genome duplication event in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome), some of which in theory may have resulted from TE movement. All-against-all best reciprocal BLASTP searches identified a total of 9,569 paralogous gene pairs. The synonymous nucleotide substitution rate (<i>K</i>
                           <sub>s</sub>) for the paralogues was calculated using codeml (PAML)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput. Appl. Biosci. &#xA;                           13, 555–556 (1997)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1902">29</a></sup>. Interestingly, the <i>K</i>
                           <sub>s</sub> distribution showed an older peak at ~0.65 and a more recent peak at ~0.25 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig2">2c</a>), suggesting that, in addition to the pan-cereal <i>rho</i> event, a larger number of dispersed genes were duplicated more recently in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome (3,034 gene pairs with <i>K</i>
                           <sub>s</sub> values less than 0.3) than other sequenced grass genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig2">2d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">16</a>). These genes were classified into gene family categories that are significantly enriched for wounding responses, endopeptidase inhibitor activity, serine-type endopeptidase inhibitor activity and enzyme inhibitor activity (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">23</a>). Careful classification of these recently duplicated gene pairs showed that 1,204 were specific to the wheat D genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">17</a>). A separate positional analysis revealed that 1,102 of the recently duplicated gene pairs are likely to have arisen via tandem duplication, and the remaining 1,932 non-tandem duplicate pairs were uniformly distributed across all chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">16</a>). We investigated the sequence similarities between the 1.5 kb flanking sequences of the 1,932 non-tandem duplicates, and found that 23 pairs had similar 5′ and 3′ flanking sequences (defined as 80% identity and 50% of the length), 273 pairs had similar 5′ flanking sequence and 46 pairs had similar 3′ flanking sequences. We further investigated if the flanking regions of these recently duplicated genes overlapped with TEs, and found that the flanking sequences of 3,415 (88%) genes had TEs. Given the tremendous number of TEs in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, and considering that less than about 36% of these resulted from tandem duplication, it seems reasonable to speculate that a burst of TE-associated gene duplication may explain the unexpectedly large number of recently duplicated genes that we observed in our <i>K</i>
                           <sub>
                    <i>s</i>
                  </sub> analysis.</p><p>Compared to other grass species, including maize, rice and <i>B. distachyon</i>, the occurrence of both retrotransposons and DNA transposons in both gene bodies and in flanking regions is highest in <i>Ae. tauschii</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig4">4a</a>). Of the predicted genes of <i>Ae. tauschii</i>, 45.5% contain at least one TE (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">21</a>), which is two times higher than in maize, another TE-rich genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science &#xA;                           326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d40588e1974">4</a></sup>. Among the 12 TE superfamilies we examined, CACTAs are the most abundant superfamily of TEs inserted in introns; these are present in 8,547 genes (43.9 % of the genes with TE insertions, Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">21</a>). We compared the length of inserted TEs in the genic regions and intergenic regions, and found that the TEs inserted in genic regions are significantly shorter than those inserted in intergenic regions (two-sample Kolmogorov–Smirnov (KS) test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.001) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">11</a>). As expected, genes containing TEs were on the whole expressed at lower levels than were genes without TEs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">18</a>, KS test, <i>P</i> &lt; 10<sup>−13</sup>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="TE distribution and methylation profiles across genes in the Ae. tauschii genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: TE distribution and methylation profiles across genes in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0067-8/MediaObjects/41477_2017_67_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0067-8/MediaObjects/41477_2017_67_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>
                                       <b>a</b>, Distributions of retrotransposons, CACTAs and other DNA transposons across gene bodies and flanking regions (±3  kb). <b>b</b>, Distributions of Gypsy elements, CACTAs and Helitrons present in expressed genes, non-expressed genes and pseudogenes. <b>c</b>, Methylation levels of expressed genes, non-expressed genes and pseudogenes. <b>d</b>, Methylation levels of TEs and their surrounding regions. The analysis in <b>d</b> included only isolated TEs (no other TE or gene within 500 bp). The meta-profile of the methylation of the first intron of all genes without TEs represents the control in this plot. TSS, transcription start site; TTS, transcription terminal site.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Pseudogenes can be regulators of biological function<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Pink, R. C. &amp; Carter, D. R. Pseudogenes as regulators of biological function. Essays in Biochemistry &#xA;                           54, 103 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2039">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Poliseno, L. et al. A coding-independent function of gene and pseudogene mRNAs regulates tumour biology. Nature &#xA;                           465, 1033–1038 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2042">31</a></sup>. To test if the high pseudogene content in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome detected above is correlated with historic bursts of TE movement in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, we identified processed pseudogenes (those resulting from retrotransposition) and examined the distribution of TEs across pseudogenes. Among the pseudogenes, about 29% of the multi-exon ancestor genes had lost their introns, suggesting that retrotransposition was involved in their pseudogenization. We also found that several superfamilies of retrotransposons and DNA TEs (for example, Gypsy, CACTAs, Helitrons, and so on, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig4">4b</a>) were enriched in pseudogene bodies and/or in the flanking regions of pseudogenes. More than 80% of the pseudogenes are somehow disrupted by TEs from these superfamilies, suggesting a pivotal role of TE movement in pseudogenization.</p><p>Cytosine DNA methylation is important in the epigenetic regulation of gene expression and in silencing transposons and other repetitive sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Law, J. A. &amp; Jacobsen, S. E. Establishing, maintaining and modifying DNA methylation patterns in plants and animals. Nat. Rev. Genet. &#xA;                           11, 204–220 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2058">32</a></sup>. To investigate DNA methylation and gene expression profiles in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, we conducted whole genome bisulfite sequencing and RNA-seq using the same sample tissues. The average percentages of methylation of CG, CHG and CHH contexts in leaf were 89.7%, 59.1% and 2.1%, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM1">24</a>). The genome CG and CHG methylation levels are about the same as those in maize (86.4%, 70.9%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, Q. &amp; Eichten, S. R. Genetic perturbation of the maize methylome. The Plant Cell &#xA;                           26, 4602–4616 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2068">33</a></sup>, and are much higher than those in <i>B. distachyon</i> (56.5%, 35.3%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Eichten, S. R. &amp; Stuart, T. DNA methylation profiles of diverse Brachypodium distachyon align with underlying genetic diversity. Genome Res. &#xA;                           26, 1520–1531 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2076">34</a></sup> and rice (44%, 24%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li, X. et al. Single-base resolution maps of cultivated and wild rice methylomes and regulatory roles of DNA methylation in plant gene expression. BMC Genomics &#xA;                           13, 300 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2080">35</a></sup>. This is consistent with previous observations indicating a positive correlation between genome size, TE content and genomic methylation levels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Alonso, C., Perez, R., Bazaga, P. &amp; Herrera, C. M. Global DNA cytosine methylation as an evolving trait: phylogenetic signal and correlated evolution with genome size in angiosperms. Front. Genet. &#xA;                           6, 4 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2084">36</a></sup>. The methylation level of CG and CHG contexts within genes is much lower than that in the intergenic regions, which might be attributable to the flanking sequences of genes in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome being frequently occupied by TEs, and typically heavily methylated (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig4">4c</a>). Similar to <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rice and maize, CG methylation is the most abundant type of methylation in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, and the CG methylation levels are low near both transcriptional start sites (TSSs) and transcriptional terminal sites (TTSs). Intriguingly, this difference is more obvious in expressed genes than in non-expressed genes, which suggests that the lower level of CG methylation around TSSs and TESs might be important in regulating the activation of gene expression. In contrast, the level of CHG methylation is higher in non-expressed genes than in expressed genes, and this trend is consistent throughout gene bodies (in both exons and introns). Regions with elevated CHH methylation levels located immediately adjacent to TSSs and TESs are referred to as ‘mCHH islands’. mCHH islands were first identified in maize, and have been proposed to function as ‘insulators’ that separate the silencing of TEs from that of nearby genes. However, there is as yet no clear conclusion about the relationship between mCHH islands and gene expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, Q. et al. RNA-directed DNA methylation enforces boundaries between heterochromatin and euchromatin in the maize genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA &#xA;                           112, 14728–14733 (2015)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2101">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gent, J. I. et al. CHH islands: de novo DNA methylation in near-gene chromatin regulation in maize. Genome Res. &#xA;                           23, 628–637 (2013)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2101_1">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Regulski, M. et al. The maize methylome influences mRNA splice sites and reveals widespread paramutation-like switches guided by small RNA. Genome Res. &#xA;                           23, 1651–1662 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2104">39</a></sup>. We found that mCHH islands are obvious in <i>Ae. tauschii</i> and, further, we found that the CHG methylation level is higher in non-expressed genes than in expressed genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig4">4c</a>), a finding that appears to provide an additional line of evidence to support this gene expression insulator theory. Our combined bisulfite sequencing and RNA-seq analysis revealed that genes with TEs inserted in their introns are expressed at lower levels than are genes without TEs, so we examined the methylation profile around TEs to test if TE insertions affect methylation of the surrounding region (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig4">4d</a>). Indeed, we found that introns with TE insertions showed higher methylation levels than introns without any TEs, although the level was lower than that for TEs in intergenic regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Integrated genetic map and key agronomic genes/QTLs map</h3><p>Extensive previous research efforts have genetically mapped a large number of agronomically important genes/QTLs. However, most of these QTLs could not be physically mapped, and individual results are not typically comparable because studies used different mapping populations and different sets of markers. To address this deficiency, we mapped all available markers, including 735 first-generation restriction-fragment length polymorphisms (RFLPs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Sharp, P. J., Chao, S., Desai, S. &amp; Gale, M. D. The isolation, characterization and application in the Triticeae of a set of wheat RFLP probes identifying each homoeologous chromosome arm. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           78, 342–348 (1989)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2125">40</a></sup>, 3,536 second-generation marker SSRs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Somers, D. J., Isaac, P. &amp; Edwards, K. A high-density microsatellite consensus map for bread wheat (Triticum aestivum L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           109, 1105–1114 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2129">41</a></sup> and millions of third-generation SNPs (90 K<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wang, S. et al. Characterization of polyploid wheat genomic diversity using a high-density 90,000 single nucleotide polymorphism array. Plant Biotechnol. J. &#xA;                           12, 787–796 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2133">42</a></sup>, 820 K<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Winfield, M. O. et al. High-density SNP genotyping array for hexaploid wheat and its secondary and tertiary gene pool. Plant Biotechnol. J. &#xA;                           14, 1195–1206 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d40588e2137">43</a></sup> and 660 K used in our laboratory) to the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, and generated a high-resolution integrated genetic map corresponding to the genome sequences (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig5">5</a>). By using this integrated genetic map, we anchored 50 genes/QTLs (with marker sequences previously detected in various populations) to the D genome. We also identified 256 agronomically important genes that have been identified by map-based cloning in cereal crops (203 from rice, 22 from wheat, 16 from barley and 15 from maize). These genes include 53 conferring disease resistance, 19 for abiotic stress tolerance, nine for domestication, 135 for development, 12 for quality and 28 for yield (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM3">25</a>). Thereby, we generated the first genome-based gene/QTL map for <i>Ae. tauschii</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#Fig5">5</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0067-8#MOESM4">26</a>). A total of 33, 54, 38, 33, 37, 20 and 58 genes/QTLs were anchored to chromosomes 1D to 7D respectively, suggesting that 2D and 7D have made relatively stronger positive contributions to wheat improvement than the other chromosomes. All of these results highlight that, in addition to its utility in genomics research, a chromosome-scale reference genome is a valuable resource for molecular breeding and gene cloning.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="A high-resolution integrated genetic map which assists anchoring agronomically important genes/QTLs of Ae. tauschii.&#xA;                                    "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: A high-resolution integrated genetic map which assists anchoring agronomically important genes/QTLs of <i>Ae. tauschii.</i>
